class="ent-tss-g7g134-blogContentWrapper"><div class="ent-tss-o5kd66-enContentEditor en-content"><div id="ent-blog-content" class="ent-tss-16bdfpw-editable" data-slate-editor="true" data-slate-node="value" contenteditable="false" zindex="-1" style="position:relative;outline:none;white-space:pre-wrap;word-wrap:break-word"><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="bottom-line-up-front"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Bottom Line Up Front</span></span></span></h2><ul data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-1jjvw86-listDisc"><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Recently, ZDNet exclusively reported a</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">leak posted on a cybercrime community containing details and credentials of over 900 enterprise Secure Pulse servers</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">exploited by threat actors</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Since this leak represents an ever-growing ransomware risk,</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">KELA delved into both the leaks content and the actors</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">who were involved in its inception and circulation</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">This short research targets a specific tier of cybercriminal actors </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Initial Access Brokers</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. These are</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">mid-tier actors who specialize in obtaining initial network access from a variety of sources, curating and grooming it into a wider network compromise</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> and then selling them off to ransomware affiliates</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">With the affiliate ransomware network becoming more and more popular and affecting huge enterprises as well as smaller ones,</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">initial access brokers are rapidly becoming an important part of the affiliate ransomware supply chain</span></strong></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">The list leak mentioned above seems to have been circulating between several initial access brokers in cybercrime forums</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, and have been exposed by a LockBit affiliate who regarded the actors as unprofessional</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">This event showcases the breadth of information thats exchanged on cybercrime communities and, in KELAs eyes,</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">emphasizes the need for scalable and targeted monitoring of underground communities</span></strong></span></span></li></ul><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="raa-s-ecosystem-101"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">RaaS Ecosystem 101</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">For the past year or so, the threat intelligence community has been closely tracking the ongoing changes to the ransomware landscape </span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.bankinfosecurity.com/experts-warn-targeted-ransomware-attacks-to-surge-a-13782" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">noting the shift in business models and TTPs</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">employed by ransom actors. While vendors may choose different terms  human-operated, multi-stage, targeted and more  they all carry the same meaning: ransomware actors are opting to go for targets that have more means and more incentive for payment, so they can demand higher ransom amounts.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">One major aspect of this trend, commonly mentioned in the media for its inherent flare and optics, is the</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.zdnet.com/article/heres-a-list-of-all-the-ransomware-gangs-who-will-steal-and-leak-your-data-if-you-dont-pay/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">naming-and-shaming</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">leveraging tactics used to further push ransomware victims to pay.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Another aspect is the</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">cooperation between actors facilitated by the rise of targeted ransomware</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">: in order to support work in scale, ransomware operators turn to partners and affiliates to fulfill their remote access needs. This cooperation results in two distinct stakeholders in the targeted ransomware-as-a-service supply chain: the ransomware developers who supply the infrastructure, and the partners who supply the network intrusion opportunities and deployment of the ransomware itself.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20390'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="The affiliate model employed by GandCrab in 2019. Source: McAfee" title="The affiliate model employed by GandCrab in 2019. Source: McAfee" width="680" height="390" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x390/1-1739438574013.jpg"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x390/1-1739438574013.jpg" alt="The affiliate model employed by GandCrab in 2019. Source: McAfee" title="The affiliate model employed by GandCrab in 2019. Source: McAfee" width="680" height="390"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">The affiliate model employed by GandCrab in 2019. Source: McAfee</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Generally speaking, the affiliates working directly with ransomware operators are highly vetted, professional actors. At any given moment, most ransomware developers are recruiting only a handful of affiliates  essentially creating a pyramid-like hierarchy; some RaaS developers are very keen on their affiliates KPIs, with underperforming affiliates being removed from the program.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20328'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Post from a cybercrime forum (accessible and translatable through DARKBEAST) where a NetWalker ransomware recruitment ad shows a very specific criteria for affiliates" title="Post from a cybercrime forum (accessible and translatable through DARKBEAST) where a NetWalker ransomware recruitment ad shows a very specific criteria for affiliates" width="680" height="328" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x328/2-1739438618482.jpg"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x328/2-1739438618482.jpg" alt="Post from a cybercrime forum (accessible and translatable through DARKBEAST) where a NetWalker ransomware recruitment ad shows a very specific criteria for affiliates" title="Post from a cybercrime forum (accessible and translatable through DARKBEAST) where a NetWalker ransomware recruitment ad shows a very specific criteria for affiliates" width="680" height="328"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">Post from a cybercrime forum (accessible and translatable through DARKBEAST) where a NetWalker ransomware recruitment ad shows a very specific criteria for affiliates</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Essentially, affiliates may be perceived as mid-level management. Facing constant scrutiny from the developers and commission-based salary thats dependent on successful network intrusions, the affiliates needs to constantly gain more network access gave rise to another actor type: the</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">initial access broker.</span></strong></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="initial-access-brokers"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Initial Access Brokers</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Initial access brokers are generally lower-tier, opportunistic actors supplying the affiliates with</span></strong></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://kelacyber.com/access-as-a-service-remote-access-markets-in-the-cybercrime-underground/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">access-as-a-service</span></strong></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">: they obtain initial access to organizations and then offer it for sale on the same underground forums occupied by RaaS affiliates.</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Affiliates then buy these initial accesses</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, pivot and move laterally until enough control is obtained so the ransomware can be spread and computers be locked.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Brokers and affiliates are not necessarily differentiated by their level of skill or expertise, but rather by the</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">monetization channels</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">they choose to employ. While affiliates or ransomware actors monetize networks by locking them and demanding ransom, initial access brokers are use-case agnostic: they sell network access to any actor  financially-motivated APTs (such as FINx groups), ransomware actors, data brokers, nation state actors trawling the cybercrime underground for leads or essentially any threat actor that has a way to monetize said access.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20402'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="A handle used by an actor associated with the access broker collective known as FXMSP, offering network access to multiple organizations on the now-defunct KickAss Forum" title="A handle used by an actor associated with the access broker collective known as FXMSP, offering network access to multiple organizations on the now-defunct KickAss Forum" width="680" height="402" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x402/32_1-1739438744403.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x402/32_1-1739438744403.png" alt="A handle used by an actor associated with the access broker collective known as FXMSP, offering network access to multiple organizations on the now-defunct KickAss Forum" title="A handle used by an actor associated with the access broker collective known as FXMSP, offering network access to multiple organizations on the now-defunct KickAss Forum" width="680" height="402"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">A handle used by an actor associated with the access broker collective known as FXMSP, offering network access to multiple organizations on the now-defunct KickAss Forum</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Monetizing network access in the cybercrime financial ecosystem is nothing new</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, and initial access brokers existed long before the rise of targeted ransomware. However, they tended to have less clients. Monetizing network access can be hard, since fraud actors  who make up the lions share of active actors on most communities  are more interested in credentials that can be easily converted to cash in hand, whereas sophisticated actors who can monetize access to an obscure network were not, up until lately, in abundance. The recently-established</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">RaaS programs have simplified the process and provided easy monetization channels for network access</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. These programs have enabled the entire chain of actors to increase profits, as high demand for network access (by affiliates) creates a higher supply of brokers dedicated to gaining network access.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">In 2019, due the rising tide of RaaS operators, threat intelligence research efforts started shifting towards access brokers as well</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. One of the notable actors to attract major attention was the loose</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.advanced-intel.com/post/top-tier-russian-hacking-collective-claims-breaches-of-three-major-anti-virus-companies" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">collective known as FXMSP</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, which operated across multiple handles and communities. Others, such as</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.recordedfuture.com/network-access-analysis/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">bc.monster or sheriff</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, are currently becoming increasingly active across Russian-speaking communities. Initial access brokers are, in fact, becoming exponentially more prevalent in cybercrime communities; the amount of posts and offers dedicated to monetizing network access has risen drastically in 2020.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20365'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Remote access offering on cybercrime forums over time. Source: Positive Technologies" title="Remote access offering on cybercrime forums over time. Source: Positive Technologies" width="680" height="365" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x365/4-1739438792618.jpg"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x365/4-1739438792618.jpg" alt="Remote access offering on cybercrime forums over time. Source: Positive Technologies" title="Remote access offering on cybercrime forums over time. Source: Positive Technologies" width="680" height="365"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">Remote access offering on cybercrime forums over time. Source: Positive Technologies</span></em></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="the-value-proposition-of-access-brokers"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The Value Proposition of Access Brokers</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Key enabling factors for the prevalence of access brokers</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">(and, indirectly, to the rapid rise in targeted ransomware)</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://kelacyber.com/access-as-a-service-remote-access-markets-in-the-cybercrime-underground/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">are the automation and servitization of cybercrime</span></strong></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Lets take Remote Desktop Protocol  a</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/ransomwares-big-jump-ransoms-grew-14-times-in-one-year/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">key vector in targeted ransomware</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. A few years ago, threat actors needed to employ a wide variety of tools to get access to a valuable, functioning RDP server belonging to a corporation  including much time spent on reconnaissance, brute forcing and nitpicking victims.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Today, one only needs to obtain an invite to one of the many Remote Access Markets and browse the continuously-updated selection of compromised machines offered for sale. One of the major RDP markets has lately also added a CORP flag  servers that are part of a corporate network are marked (and priced) differently, so actors can easily spot them and get the most bang for their RDP buck.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20483'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="An RDP server belonging to an organization offered for sale on an automated market for USD 40" title="An RDP server belonging to an organization offered for sale on an automated market for USD 40" width="680" height="483" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x483/52-1739438863481.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x483/52-1739438863481.png" alt="An RDP server belonging to an organization offered for sale on an automated market for USD 40" title="An RDP server belonging to an organization offered for sale on an automated market for USD 40" width="680" height="483"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">This wide selection of goods allows actors a higher volume of work</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. For a few hundred dollars, initial access brokers can acquire multiple RDP corporate servers; from that selection they can groom the ones that look interesting  perform some reconnaissance, escalate privileges or install further tooling. Once a target is ripe and ready, it can be offered for thousands of dollars on cybercrime markets where ransomware affiliates can acquire it and move forward with the final attack.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20198'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="a diagram of remote access markets/campaigns" title="Remote access markets/campaigns" width="680" height="198" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x198/6_1-1739438898106.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x198/6_1-1739438898106.png" alt="a diagram of remote access markets/campaigns" title="Remote access markets/campaigns" width="680" height="198"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">This model creates different stakeholders in the supply chain  each with their own expertise, scale of operations and profit margins. During each step of the process both the scope and price of the access get bigger  yielding profits to each stakeholder, and creating small, self-contained ecosystems that can support large-scale cybercrime efforts.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="the-secret-life-of-an-initial-access-broker"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The Secret Life of an Initial Access Broker</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">On August 4, 2020, ZDNet, in collaboration with KELAs tools,</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.zdnet.com/article/hacker-leaks-passwords-for-900-enterprise-vpn-servers/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">exclusively reported</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">on a threat actor exposing a list of over 900 compromised Secure Pulse VPN servers  including credentials, session keys and other sensitive information being exposed. This was an interesting event for several reasons. First and foremost, the actual leakage provided visibility into targeted servers being used as potential ransomware targets  which has great intelligence monitoring value. Second, the leak  and the story behind it  provides an interesting behind-the-scenes look into the activity on an initial access broker.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">First, in order to understand the story behind the recent leak, one needs to get familiar with the threat actors involved. The first  a man from Ukraine who, according to his posts, started his hacking activities in 2016  goes by the handle </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Bassterlord</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. According to the actors own accounts, after launching spam campaigns designed to deliver trojans and infostealers, he switched to targeting organizations under the mentorship of an unknown threat actor who hired him in 2019 and provided counselling.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">In a competition of hacking-related articles held on a Russian-speaking underground forum, Bassterlord shared a writeup of his original story, of how his mentor offered him around $300 a month to send spam emails containing malicious attachments to banking and financial organizations. The actors have targeted 12,000 companies over almost one year, though they managed to infect only two of them. Nothing worked for me, I was desperate, shared Bassterlord in this write-up. This was when his mentor taught him how to compromise organizations via RDP. At the same time, Bassterlord claims that his mentor got access to Sodinokibi  probably referring to the mentor becoming a Sodinokibi affiliate, showcasing the typical relationship between an affiliate and a soon-to-be initial access broker. Eager to get more money, Bassterlord switched to another TTP.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Bassterlords current primary TTP involves the use of an</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://github.com/BishopFox/pwn-pulse" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">automated script</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">weaponizing CVE-2019-11510, an arbitrary file read vulnerability affecting Pulse Connect Secure SSL VPN, which has lately become</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://us-cert.cisa.gov/ncas/alerts/aa20-010a" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">popular with ransomware actors</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. Moreover, he states that he continues to work with his mentor and former employer, which is apparently still involved in Sodinokibi operations.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20331'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Screenshot of Bassterlord " title="Screenshot of Bassterlord " width="680" height="331" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x331/7-1739439209522.jpg"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x331/7-1739439209522.jpg" alt="Screenshot of Bassterlord " title="Screenshot of Bassterlord " width="680" height="331"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Another threat actor involved in the story is named </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">uhodiransomwar</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> (from Russian  Go away, ransomware), previously known as m1x. He is a veteran actor, active since 2009 (though freshly banned) on the same underground forum as Bassterlord. A major part of uhodiransomwars latest activity is small-scale ransomware: he mostly shares data from organizations he claimed to breach once no payment is received. It seems that he sometimes acts independently, and is also self-associated with known RaaS actors  such as</span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.cynet.com/blog/threat-report-lockbit-ransomware/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">LockBit</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20269'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="A comment in the official LockBit affiliate recruitment thread by uhodiransomwar, claiming to have access to the affiliates panel" title="A comment in the official LockBit affiliate recruitment thread by uhodiransomwar, claiming to have access to the affiliates panel" width="680" height="269" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x269/8-1739442737145.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x269/8-1739442737145.png" alt="A comment in the official LockBit affiliate recruitment thread by uhodiransomwar, claiming to have access to the affiliates panel" title="A comment in the official LockBit affiliate recruitment thread by uhodiransomwar, claiming to have access to the affiliates panel" width="680" height="269"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">A comment in the official LockBit affiliate recruitment thread by uhodiransomwar, claiming to have access to the affiliates panel</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">In the last few weeks, Bassterlord and uhodiransomwar have been publicly clashing in the forum  showing the contrasting views of a novice initial access broker and a ransomware affiliate, respectively. These ongoing clashes have led to the credentials leak mentioned above.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="setting-the-stage-a-not-so-great-initial-access-broker"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Setting the Stage: a Not-So-Great Initial Access Broker</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">On July 27, 2020, Bassterlord offered network access to a US-based, state-level government organization for USD 8,000. The post claimed that the initial vector was VPN access which then was leveraged into RDP. First responder to the thread was uhodiransomwar, who claimed that the infiltrated network was shared between a few different actors  implying that the backdoor Bassterlord was offering wasnt exclusive and that he himself has access to the server already, and therefore may be worth less than the price asked.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20431'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Google-translated post where  Bassterlord recently posted access to a government entity in the US for sale. " title="Google-translated post where  Bassterlord recently posted access to a government entity in the US for sale. " width="680" height="431" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x431/9-1739442811178.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x431/9-1739442811178.png" alt="Google-translated post where  Bassterlord recently posted access to a government entity in the US for sale. " title="Google-translated post where  Bassterlord recently posted access to a government entity in the US for sale. " width="680" height="431"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">Google-translated post where  Bassterlord recently posted access to a government entity in the US for sale. </span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The actors continued their public discussion, with Bassterlord admitting that the initial VPN access was indeed public knowledge  implying that multiple actors knew about it to begin with. He later made the case that even though multiple actors may be aware of the access itself, hes not aware of the inner workings on the network  only to his own access, leveraged into RDP. He also claims that in his eyes, network access  even if shared  should be first come, first serve: the first actor to leverage it into a full-blown attack gets the spoils.</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">This exemplifies the role of an initial access broker in the ransomware supply chain</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">: they are the function that can take an initial lead to a vulnerability, and work it into a full blown network access. Thus, several actors working on the same initial access may come up with different results  creating an arms race between brokers and the affiliates who drive them.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">This dialogue, though appears to be superficial, showcases that uhodiransomwar was able to identify the victim only through Bassterlords description of it  meaning he had actual access to it as well. This fact implies that, somewhere,</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">initial access brokers and affiliates may have access to some shared database of breached entities.</span></strong></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Very quickly this proved to indeed be the case, as on August 3, 2020, uhodiransomwar published the aforementioned Pulse Secure leak. In his main post, the actor claimed to be tired of other actors posing these so-called public compromises as private access to corporations and trying to profit off of them  hinting towards Bassterlords latest offers.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="bassterlords-little-black-book"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Bassterlords Little Black Book</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Upon investigating the content of the leak, KELA was quickly able to match against several network accesses offered by the actor  proving that the leak indeed was the source of multiple (if not all) of Bassterlords offers as an initial access broker. Specifically,</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">we were able to trace servers back to the government entity mentioned in the paragraph above</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> as well as another of Bassterlords victims:</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">a university to which he sold access for USD 12,000</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">on July 12.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">After identifying several victims, we tried to get to the bottom of the list; where did it originate, and was it really passed between actors who were discreetly sharing compromises? Digging a bit back into previous posts by the actor, we found a reference in which</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Bassterlord stated that he has potential access to at least 77 organizations</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">known to be vulnerable. In this thread, Bassterlord, somewhat braggingly, likened himself to Andrey Turchin  the main figure behind the notorious initial access broker FXMSP  stating that in the news, they write that he [FXMSP  KELA] got access to more than 130 corporations. So Im almost caught up with him. To this post, Bassterlord attached a heavily redacted screenshot of an Excel file that seems to contain dozens of entries, each one representing an initial entry vector (assumed to be a vulnerable Pulse Secure server) to an organization.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20502'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Bassterlords post and a snapshot of his access list" title="Bassterlords post and a snapshot of his access list" width="680" height="502" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x502/10-1739443538160.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x502/10-1739443538160.png" alt="Bassterlords post and a snapshot of his access list" title="Bassterlords post and a snapshot of his access list" width="680" height="502"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">Bassterlords post and a snapshot of his access list</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The fact that Bassterlord was maintaining an active list of VPN-based accesses, coupled with the fact that uhodiransomwar claimed to have knowledge and access to it, implies that some version of this list was being shared or circulated internally between actors.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">At times it seems that Bassterlord was trying to break out of the initial access broker cage and monetize his network accesses via direct extortion demands</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. A now-deleted tweet from July 11 by the actor taunts a Spain-based firm to pay within 5 days, or risk GDPR fines. However, its unclear whether this claimed attack was carried out via actual ransomware or manual extortion. Another hint to Bassterlords attempts to deploy ransomware is his post from July 12, where he discontinued the sale of access to a Thailand media company because it was locked by me [him] personally.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20592'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="a tweet from Bassterlord" width="680" height="592" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x592/11-1739443611719.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x592/11-1739443611719.png" alt="a tweet from Bassterlord" width="680" height="592"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-yjupvi-expandIcon ent-tss-45-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div> <div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="whats-in-a-list"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Whats in a List?</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The full files leaked by uhodiransomwar and covered by ZDNet were in fact an archive, containing over 900 txt files  each pertaining to an exploited Secure Pulse server, and accompanied with notes taken by the actor who compiled the archive.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">For example, 49 of the 900+ servers are noted to require two factor authentication in order to login to the admin panel:</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:center"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20255'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Raw notes from the leaked archive, mentioning servers with 2FA" title="Raw notes from the leaked archive, mentioning servers with 2FA" width="680" height="255" data-slate-length="0"></span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">Raw notes from the leaked archive, mentioning servers with 2FA</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Of the remaining servers, around 250 are noted to be bad, invalid or otherwise inaccessible  and over 150 are noted as need hash (probably referring to a lack of a key data point  a password hash or a key  within the data obtained from the server, probably rendering it not as easily accessible).</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Overall, it seems that around 400 of the servers included in the list are potentially valid and can be leveraged to deploy ransomware. Of these, some have notes mentioning the name of the company and, quite commonly, the organizations revenue as well:</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20255'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Notes mentioning potential victims revenue in a shortened notation. For example, 17m (row 7) means USD 17 million, and 3b means USD 3 billionNotes mentioning potential victims revenue in a shortened notation. For example, 17m (row 7) means USD 17 million, and 3b means USD 3 billion" title="Notes mentioning potential victims revenue in a shortened notation. For example, 17m (row 7) means USD 17 million, and 3b means USD 3 billion" width="680" height="255" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x255/13-1739443823899.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x255/13-1739443823899.png" alt="Notes mentioning potential victims revenue in a shortened notation. For example, 17m (row 7) means USD 17 million, and 3b means USD 3 billion</span></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Sifting through these notes, one can easily trace several multi-million dollar companies  several of them public  as well as a few with over a billion USD in revenue.</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">These findings showcase the threat level that big, seemingly well-protected organizations may face from relatively novice actors acting as initial access brokers</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Researching victims revenue is a very common practice for initial access brokers, whose posts lately tend to mention revenue of their victims in order to entice potential buyers  based on an assumption that organizations with higher revenues would have the potential for a multi-million dollar ransom.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-55ybit-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The real question to try and answer is:</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">How many of the entries in the archive made it past initial access brokers to ransomware affiliates  suffering a full-blown ransomware attack.</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Here, the main obstacle in finding an answer is the sheer amount of ransomware attacks that go unreported by the victims.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Still, we were able to track leads to several companies that are publicly known to have suffered a breach that were mentioned in the files. For example, the file contains a mention of a company known to have impacted by a naming-and-shaming ransom actor; while we cant corroborate a direct cause-and-effect relationship here, this leak still serves as a reminder that monitoring cybercrime sources can prove crucial for organizations.</span></span></span></p></div></div></div></article><div
